Arenas-Gamboa, <email>aarenas@cvm.tamu.edu</email></corresp><fn fn-type="other" id="fn004"><p>This article was submitted to Infectious Diseases, a section of the journal Frontiers in Microbiology</p></fn></author-notes><pub-date pub-type="epub"><day>15</day><month>7</month><year>2021</year></pub-date><pub-date pub-type="collection"><year>2021</year></pub-date><volume>12</volume><issue-id pub-id-type="pmc-issue-id">374661</issue-id><elocation-id>713157</elocation-id><history><date date-type="received"><day>21</day><month>5</month><year>2021</year></date><date date-type="accepted"><day>22</day><month>6</month><year>2021</year></date></history><pub-history><event event-type="pmc-release"><date><day>15</day><month>07</month><year>2021</year></date></event><event event-type="pmc-live"><date><day>30</day><month>07</month><year>2021</year></date></event><event event-type="pmc-last-change"><date iso-8601-date="2021-08-02 16:16:30.027"><day>02</day><month>08</month><year>2021</year></date></event></pub-history><permissions><copyright-statement>Copyright &#169; 2021 Stranahan and Arenas-Gamboa.</copyright-statement><copyright-year>2021</copyright-year><copyright-holder>Stranahan and Arenas-Gamboa</copyright-holder><license><ali:license_ref specific-use="textmining" content-type="ccbylicense">https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/</ali:license_ref><license-p>This is an open-access article distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License (CC BY). The use, distribution or reproduction in other forums is permitted, provided the original author(s) and the copyright owner(s) are credited and that the original publication in this journal is cited, in accordance with accepted academic practice. No use, distribution or reproduction is permitted which does not comply with these terms.</license-p></license></permissions><self-uri xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" content-type="pmc-pdf" xlink:href="fmicb-12-713157.pdf"/><abstract><p><italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> is a facultatively intracellular bacterial pathogen and the cause of worldwide zoonotic infections, infamous for its ability to evade the immune system and persist chronically within host cells. Despite the frequent association with attenuation in other Gram-negative bacteria, a rough lipopolysaccharide phenotype is retained by <italic toggle="yes">Brucella canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">Brucella ovis</italic>, which remain fully virulent in their natural canine and ovine hosts, respectively. While these natural rough strains lack the O-polysaccharide they, like their smooth counterparts, are able to evade and manipulate the host immune system by exhibiting low endotoxic activity, resisting destruction by complement and antimicrobial peptides, entering and trafficking within host cells along a similar pathway, and interfering with MHC-II antigen presentation. <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> appear to have compensated for their roughness by alterations to their outer membrane, especially in regards to outer membrane proteins. <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, in particular, also shows evidence of being less proinflammatory <italic toggle="yes">in vivo</italic>, suggesting that the rough phenotype may be associated with an enhanced level of stealth that could allow these pathogens to persist for longer periods of time undetected. Nevertheless, much additional work is required to understand the correlates of immune protection against the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., a critical step toward development of much-needed vaccines. This review will highlight the significance of rough lipopolysaccharide in the context of both natural disease and host&#8211;pathogen interactions with an emphasis on natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and the implications for vaccine development.</p></abstract><kwd-group><kwd><italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic></kwd><kwd>lipopolysaccharides (LPS)</kwd><kwd>O-polysaccharide</kwd><kwd>vaccine</kwd><kwd>bacteria</kwd><kwd>Gram-negative</kwd><kwd>host&#8211;pathogen id="S1"><title>Introduction</title><p>Despite knowledge of its existence for over a century, brucellosis remains one of the most commonly reported zoonotic diseases worldwide (<xref rid="B126" ref-type="bibr">Pappas et al., 2006</xref>). A significant contributor to this is the fact that the causative Gram-negative bacterium, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>, is facultatively intracellular. This feature allows <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to deviously persist within the host&#8217;s cells where it can evade many components of the immune system. On top of this, numerous animal species, particularly livestock, can carry the organism and readily transmit it to humans. Of the 12 identified species, the most frequently reported causes of human infection are <italic toggle="yes">Brucella melitensis, Brucella abortus</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">Brucella suis</italic> with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> associated with fewer reported cases (<xref rid="B38" ref-type="bibr">Corbel, 1997</xref>; <xref rid="B125" ref-type="bibr">Pappas et al., 2008</xref>). Although cross-species infections are common with the first three strains, each shows a strong host preference with disease usually occurring in small ruminants, cattle, pigs, and dogs, respectively. <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. may also be classified according to their lipopolysaccharide (LPS) phenotype. The &#8220;classical&#8221; strains most commonly associated with human infection, <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis, B. abortus</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">B. suis</italic>, exhibit a smooth LPS while <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> is naturally rough (<xref rid="B180" ref-type="bibr">Whatmore, 2009</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> owes this designation to the fact that its LPS conspicuously lacks the terminal O-polysaccharide (O-PS). Interestingly, roughness is not unique to <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> as <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, virulent in sheep, also lacks O-PS.</p><p>The rough phenotype of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, defined by a lack of O-PS, is unusual as this trait is typically associated with attenuation in Gram-negative bacteria, yet both are fully virulent in their natural hosts (<xref rid="B119" ref-type="bibr">Moreno et al., 1984</xref>; <xref rid="B54" ref-type="bibr">Erridge et al., 2002</xref>). Like their smooth counterparts, both can cause reproductive disease in these species. In bitches, disease manifests as abortion while males commonly exhibit prostatitis and epididymitis (<xref rid="B20" ref-type="bibr">Carmichael and Kenney, 1968</xref>; <xref rid="B115" ref-type="bibr">Moore and Kakuk, 1969</xref>). Less commonly, dogs may present with diskospondylitis or uveitis (<xref rid="B92" ref-type="bibr">Kerwin et al., 1992</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, on the other hand, is a frequent cause of chronic epididymitis, orchitis, and infertility in rams with occasional induction of abortion in ewes (<xref rid="B17" ref-type="bibr">Buddle, 1956</xref>; <xref rid="B64" ref-type="bibr">Ficapal et al., 1998</xref>). One critical difference between these two strains, however, is that <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> is zoonotic while <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> is not (<xref rid="B38" ref-type="bibr">Corbel, 1997</xref>).</p><p>The importance of developing new safe and effective vaccines against brucellosis cannot be overstated and this critical need is one aspect that applies to both smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. despite the difference in LPS phenotype. While canine brucellosis has historically been considered a pathogen predominantly of kenneled dogs, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> has been isolated with increasing frequency from stray dog populations as well as pets throughout the world (<xref rid="B83" ref-type="bibr">Hensel et al., 2018</xref>; <xref rid="B177" ref-type="bibr">Wang et al., 2018</xref>; <xref rid="B164" ref-type="bibr">Su&#225;rez-Esquivel et al., 2021</xref>). Although <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> is perceived to be less virulent for humans, manifestation of human disease can occasionally be severe (<xref rid="B132" ref-type="bibr">Piampiano et al., 2000</xref>; <xref rid="B81" ref-type="bibr">Gul et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B112" ref-type="bibr">Marzetti et al., 2013</xref>). Detection of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> in a kennel can also be devastating as the pathogen is highly contagious between dogs and those infected are frequently euthanized. <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, although not zoonotic, can still result in significant economic losses for sheep farmers (<xref rid="B13" ref-type="bibr">Blasco, 1990</xref>). Unfortunately, no vaccine is currently available to protect against <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> infection in dogs and the use of the commercially available Rev.1 vaccine, protective against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in sheep, is not approved for use in areas free of <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic>. Comprehending the components of the immune response required to protect against these naturally rough pathogens is crucial for development of new and effective vaccines.</p><p>This review will highlight the significance of rough LPS in the context of both natural disease and interaction with the immune response with an emphasis on natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and the implications for vaccine development.</p></sec><sec id="S2"><title>Lipopolysaccharide Structure</title><p>As with most Gram-negative bacteria, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. produce an LPS that plays a critical role in maintaining outer membrane integrity and survival within the host (<xref rid="B54" ref-type="bibr">Erridge et al., 2002</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>). The classical smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> LPS is composed of three main components: (1) lipid A; (2) a polysaccharide core; and (3) O-PS composed of repeating glycosyl subunits (<xref rid="B159" ref-type="bibr">Smith, 2018</xref>; <xref ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>). Lipid A is responsible for most of the endotoxic activity of LPS and its hydrophobicity allows it to anchor LPS to the outer membrane (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>). Not only does lipid A play this key structural role, but it is the component of LPS that is recognized by Toll-like receptor (TLR)-4, one of the ways in which host immune cells can identify Gram-negative bacteria (<xref rid="B101" ref-type="bibr">Maldonado et al., 2016</xref>). In <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>, lipid A is composed of a diaminoglucose backbone and is distinguished by a preponderance of C16 and C18 fatty acids as well as C28 and other very long chain fatty acids (VLCFAs), an unusual feature which results in a bulkier structure than most other Gram-negative bacteria (<xref rid="B85" ref-type="bibr">Iriarte et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>).</p><fig id="F1" position="float" orientation="portrait"><label>FIGURE 1</label><caption><p>Schematic of the structure of lipopolysaccharide (LPS) of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in comparison to other Gram-negative bacteria. <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> LPS is unusual in several ways, including the incorporation of very long chain fatty acids (VLCFAs) in the lipid A and a core oligosaccharide side branch composed of several glucosamine residues. In contrast, most other Gram-negative bacteria, best exemplified by <italic toggle="yes">Escherichia coli</italic>, possess a more compact lipid A and no core side branches. <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. may be categorized as smooth if they possess an intact O-polysaccharide (O-PS), as is the case with most zoonotic strains. Alternatively, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">Brucella ovis</italic> are naturally rough, completely lacking the O-PS. While typically associated with attenuation, both rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. remain fully virulent in their natural hosts, as does the naturally rough <italic toggle="yes">Yersinia pestis</italic>. D, diaminoglucose; G, glucose; Ga, galactose; Gln, glucosamine; H, heptose; 3-deoxy-<sc>D</sc>-mannose-2-octulosonic acid, Kdo; M, mannose; N, <italic toggle="yes">N</italic>-acetylglucosamine; Pe, <italic toggle="yes">N</italic>-formylperosamine; Q, quinovosamine. Adapted from <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla (2015)</xref>, <xref rid="B182" ref-type="bibr">Yang et al. (2019)</xref>, and <xref rid="B157" ref-type="bibr">Singh et al. (2020)</xref>. Created with <ext-link xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="http://www.Biorender.com">Biorender.com</ext-link>.</p></caption><graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="float" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="fmicb-12-713157-g001.jpg"/></fig><p>The core oligosaccharide serves as a bridge between lipid A and O-PS and is important in maintaining the stability and rigidity of the outer membrane (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>). In <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., the core is composed of two 3-deoxy-<sc>D</sc>-manno-2-octulosonic acid (Kdo) sugars, one of which is linked to the O-PS (<xref rid="B114" ref-type="bibr">Monreal et al., 2003</xref>). The other Kdo notably forms a unique branching side chain composed of glucosamine, glucose, and mannose (<xref rid="B75" ref-type="bibr">Gil-Ram&#237;rez et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B66" ref-type="bibr">Fontana et al., 2016</xref>). As will be discussed in further detail below, the side branch appears to play an important role in survival within the host, helping to reduce immune recognition by TLR4 and enhancing resistance to complement and bactericidal peptides (<xref rid="B35" ref-type="bibr">Conde-&#193;lvarez et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B160" ref-type="bibr">Soler-Llor&#233;ns et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B66" ref-type="bibr">Fontana et al., 2016</xref>). Although the structure of natural <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> LPS has not been fully elucidated, the overall structure of the core oligosaccharide with side chain attached to a bulky lipid A embedded in the outer membrane is presumed to be similar if not the same between naturally smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp.</p><p>The O-PS, a key virulence factor in smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., remains the most studied component of this pathogen&#8217;s LPS (<xref rid="B3" ref-type="bibr">Allen et al., 1998</xref>; <xref rid="B89" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>; <xref ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>). The lack of O-PS is what differentiates the LPS of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> from their smooth counterparts. In other Gram-negative bacteria, the O-PS typically exhibits a high degree of variability, allowing for strain differentiation by molecular methods (<xref rid="B178" ref-type="bibr">Wang et al., 2010</xref>; <xref rid="B159" ref-type="bibr">Smith, 2018</xref>). In contrast, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> O-PS is highly homogeneous, possibly due to its niche within the intracellular environment limiting horizontal transfer with other bacteria, as suggested by <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla (2015)</xref>. The <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> O-PS is composed of homopolymers of <italic toggle="yes">N</italic>-formylperosamine with minor variations depending on strain, in which &#8220;A-dominant&#8221; strains exhibit a linear &#945;-1,2-linked polymer with approximately 2% &#945;-1,3 linkages while the O-PS from &#8220;M-dominant&#8221; strains is a linear polymer of tetrasaccharide repeating units containing one &#945;-1,3 linkage and three &#945;-1,2-linked monosaccharide residues (<xref rid="B113" ref-type="bibr">Meikle et al., 1989</xref>; <xref rid="B94" ref-type="bibr">Kubler-Kielb and Vinogradov, 2013</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>). Although not sufficient for speciation, serotyping using monoclonal antibodies directed against A or M epitopes can allow for distinction of different biovars within a particular smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> species (<xref rid="B4" ref-type="bibr">Alton et al., 1988</xref>). Further discussion of O-PS in this review will focus on the classical smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. (<italic toggle="yes">B. abortus, B. melitensis</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">B. suis</italic>). Although more recently identified <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> species have been characterized as smooth (<italic toggle="yes">B. neotomae, B. ceti, B. pinnipedialis, B. microti, B. papionis</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">B. inopinata</italic>), the complete structure of their LPS, including the O-PS, remains to be elucidated. Preliminary work has suggested that some of these strains not only lack typical O-PS epitopes but also suspected alterations to the core and/or lipid A, possibly accounting for a lack of zoonotic potential in the majority of these species (<xref rid="B34" ref-type="bibr">Cloeckaert et al., 1998</xref>; <xref rid="B11" ref-type="bibr">Baucheron et al., 2002</xref>; <xref rid="B186" ref-type="bibr">Zygmunt et al., 2012</xref>).</p><p>The mechanisms of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> O-PS and core oligosaccharide synthesis have been extensively reviewed (<xref rid="B18" ref-type="bibr">Cardoso et al., 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>) and the genes involved are summarized in <xref rid="T1" ref-type="table">Table 1</xref>. Interestingly, the lack of O-PS in <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> appears to have evolved separately in an example of convergent evolution and the mutations resulting in their rough phenotypes are completely different (<xref rid="B175" ref-type="bibr">Vizca&#237;no et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B185" ref-type="bibr">Zygmunt et al., 2009</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, for instance, lacks the entire genomic island-2 (GI-2) encompassing the key glycosyltransferases <italic toggle="yes">wboA</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">wboB</italic>, while <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> retains this region (<xref rid="B175" ref-type="bibr">Vizca&#237;no et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B169" ref-type="bibr">Tsolis et al., 2009</xref>).</p><table-wrap id="T1" position="float" orientation="portrait"><label>TABLE 1</label><caption><p>Genes involved in the synthesis of the core oligosaccharide and O-polysaccharide in <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp.</p></caption><table frame="hsides" rules="groups" cellspacing="5" cellpadding="5"><thead><tr><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">LPS component</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Gene(s)</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Function</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Comments</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">References</td></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">O-PS</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wboA</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Glycosyltransferase, O-PS synthesis</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Encoded on genomic island-2, absent in <italic toggle="yes">B.</italic> ovis; Disrupted in RB51</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B138" ref-type="bibr">Rajashekara et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B104" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla et al., 2010</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wboB</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Glycosyltransferase, O-PS synthesis</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Encoded on genomic island-2, absent in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B138" ref-type="bibr">Rajashekara et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B104" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla et al., 2010</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wbkA, wbkE</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">O-PS polymerization</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B185" ref-type="bibr">Zygmunt et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">gmd, per</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Perosamine synthesis</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B77" ref-type="bibr">Godfroid et al., 1998</xref>, <xref rid="B76" ref-type="bibr">2000</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wbkC</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">N</italic>-formylation of perosamine residues</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wbkD, wbkF</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Bactoprenol priming</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Both disrupted in <italic toggle="yes">B. canis wbkD</italic> disrupted in RB51</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B185" ref-type="bibr">Zygmunt et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B16" ref-type="bibr">Bricker et al., 2020</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wzm, wzt</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">O-PS transport to outer membrane</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wzt</italic> truncated in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B175" ref-type="bibr">Vizca&#237;no et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B179" ref-type="bibr">Wattam et al., 2009</xref></td></tr><tr><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Core</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">wa**</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Glycosyltransferase</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B120" ref-type="bibr">Moriy&#243;n et al., 2004</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">pgm</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Phosphoglucomutase</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B172" ref-type="bibr">Ugalde et al., 2000</xref></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">wadB, wadC, wadD</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Glycosyltransferase</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Synthesis of the branching side chain</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B75" ref-type="bibr">Gil-Ram&#237;rez et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B148" ref-type="bibr">Salvador-Besc&#243;s et al., 2018</xref></td></tr></tbody></table></table-wrap><p>Interestingly, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> exhibits additional genetic differences from the smooth strains not identified in <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, such as the <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> pathogenicity island 1 (BOPI-1) which includes 28 open reading frames (ORFs) absent in other <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> species and is required for <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> pathogenesis (<xref rid="B169" ref-type="bibr">Tsolis et al., 2009</xref>). At least some of these genes, such as the ABC transporter system, appear to compensate for a lack of alternative nutrient import pathways in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> caused by separate mutations (<xref rid="B156" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2011</xref>, <xref rid="B155" ref-type="bibr">2014</xref>). Whether additional compensation for lack of O-PS is provided by these genes remains to be determined. <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> are also distinguished by a higher number of pseudogenes, with the greatest number reported in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, a sign indicative of genome degradation (<xref rid="B169" ref-type="bibr">Tsolis et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B179" ref-type="bibr">Wattam et al., 2009</xref>). It has been suggested that the narrower host range of these natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. is related to this process (<xref rid="B169" ref-type="bibr">Tsolis et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). However, the possibility of additional gain-of-function mutations leading to enhanced tropism for particular organ systems in their natural hosts despite a lack of O-PS (i.e., male genital tract for <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>) cannot be excluded (<xref rid="B169" ref-type="bibr">Tsolis et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B117" ref-type="bibr">Moreno, 2014</xref>).</p><p>The indication that loss of O-PS occurred on two separate occasions amongst <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. suggests that this provided an evolutionary adaptation to <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and/or these two strains were able to compensate for its loss by additional but separate changes. This is a mystery within the field of brucellosis that is not yet unraveled but insights can be gained by understanding the roles O-PS plays for smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and how the natural rough strains compare in this regard.</p><p>Although separate from the LPS, outer membrane proteins (Omps) are another structural feature of the <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> outer membrane that deserves special mention. Extensively reviewed elsewhere, Omps have been shown to be critical to outer membrane stability and appear to be especially important for the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. (<xref rid="B33" ref-type="bibr">Cloeckaert et al., 2002</xref>; <xref rid="B145" ref-type="bibr">Roop et al., 2021</xref>). As will be discussed in greater detail later, certain Omps have been shown to contribute to complement and antimicrobial peptide resistance, to be essential for internalization of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> into cells, and to inhibit antigen presentation (<xref rid="B21" ref-type="bibr">Caro-Hern&#225;ndez et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B10" ref-type="bibr">Barrionuevo et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B108" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2008</xref>). Interestingly, the pattern of Omp expression, including Omp25/Omp31, in natural rough strains appears to differ from one another and with that of the smooth strains, although there is also variation amongst the classical smooth strains (<xref rid="B175" ref-type="bibr">Vizca&#237;no et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B109" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2009</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic>, for instance, lacks Omp31 and Omp25b, while <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> lacks Omp31b (<xref rid="B33" ref-type="bibr">Cloeckaert et al., 2002</xref>). Additionally, while <italic toggle="yes">omp25</italic> deletion mutants of <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> are attenuated in mice, this reduction in virulence is markedly more pronounced in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, although a separate study noted no attenuation in a <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> mutant (<xref rid="B51" ref-type="bibr">Edmonds et al., 2002</xref>; <xref rid="B106" ref-type="bibr">Manterola et al., 2007</xref>).</p></sec><sec id="S3"><title>The Biological Significance of LPS</title><p>The O-PS of the classical smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. is a well-characterized virulence factor (<xref rid="B96" ref-type="bibr">Lapaque et al., 2005</xref>; <xref rid="B159" ref-type="bibr">Smith, 2018</xref>). The following sections will refer to rough mutants which are derived from smooth parent strains by deletion of any number of genes involved in O-PS synthesis and should be distinguished from natural rough <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>. The majority of studies assessing the function of O-PS focus on comparing smooth strains and their rough mutants. Thus, far more is known about the relevance of the rough phenotype in rough mutants rather than in the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. Nevertheless, natural rough strains appear to more closely mimic their smooth counterparts than rough mutants in features and interactions typically associated with expression of O-PS, such as resistance to complement, cellular entry and trafficking, and lack of induction of cytotoxicity and proinflammatory cytokine production, as summarized in <xref ref-type="fig" rid="F2">Figure 2</xref>.</p><fig id="F2" position="float" orientation="portrait"><label>FIGURE 2</label><caption><p>Functions of lipopolysaccharide (LPS) in <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in comparison between smooth, rough, and rough mutant strains. Smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. are known to be far more resistant to destruction by complement while rough mutants derived from these strains, in which the O-polysaccharide (O-PS) is not produced, are generally susceptible. Natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., such as <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, exhibit complement resistance as high or higher than their smooth counterparts despite a lack of O-PS with additional components such as the core oligosaccharide side branch and outer membrane proteins (Omps) making contributions. An additional difference is the ability of smooth and natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to enter cells using lipid rafts and replicate intracellularly while rough mutants are typically unable to do this. Smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. use O-PS as a ligand for the scavenger receptor, SR-A on host cells while natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. are thought to use outer membrane proteins for this interaction. Finally, rough mutants are known to cause cytotoxicity following cell infection and induce significant proinflammatory cytokine production while natural smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. do not. Such changes indicate that natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. are able to compensate for the loss of O-PS during infection. Created with <ext-link xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="http://www.Biorender.com">Biorender.com</ext-link>.</p></caption><graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="float" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="fmicb-12-713157-g002.jpg"/></fig><sec id="S3.SS1"><title>Low Endotoxic Activity</title><p>Compared to most other Gram-negative bacteria, the LPS of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> is well known for exhibiting remarkably low endotoxin activity, being several hundred times less toxic than <italic toggle="yes">Escherichia coli</italic> LPS (<xref rid="B79" ref-type="bibr">Goldstein et al., 1992</xref>; <xref rid="B49" ref-type="bibr">Due&#241;as et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B170" ref-type="bibr">Tumurkhuu et al., 2006</xref>). The low endotoxic activity of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> LPS is related to the structure of the lipid A and core. Endotoxicity is generally associated with the acyl chain number and length of lipid A, which is recognized by host cell Toll-like receptor 4 (TLR4) and its co-receptor MD2 (<xref rid="B54" ref-type="bibr">Erridge et al., 2002</xref>). The preponderance of VLCFAs in <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> lipid A results in a bulky molecule that binds poorly with MD2 (<xref rid="B96" ref-type="bibr">Lapaque et al., 2005</xref>). In recent years, an additional explanation for the poor endotoxic activity of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> LPS has been revealed following the discovery of the lateral core oligosaccharide side branch.</p><p>Mutation of <italic toggle="yes">wadC</italic>, <italic toggle="yes">wadB</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">wadD</italic>, glycosyltransferases required for the branch&#8217;s synthesis, has demonstrated the important roles this structure plays (<xref rid="B35" ref-type="bibr">Conde-&#193;lvarez et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B94" ref-type="bibr">Kubler-Kielb and Vinogradov, 2013</xref>). The side chain imparts a positive charge onto the core oligosaccharide that shields the more internal negative charges of the inner core and lipid A, thereby preventing effective interaction with TLR4/MD2 on host macrophages and dendritic cells (<xref rid="B35" ref-type="bibr">Conde-&#193;lvarez et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B66" ref-type="bibr">Fontana et al., 2016</xref>). This is important to note as the side chain is independent of the O-PS and thus likely performs the same function for the natural rough strains. Evidence toward this is the fact that purified LPS from <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> exhibits low endotoxin-specific <italic toggle="yes">Limulus</italic> activity comparable to LPS of <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> and significantly less than that of the classic endotoxic LPS of <italic toggle="yes">E. coli</italic> (<xref rid="B119" ref-type="bibr">Moreno et al., 1984</xref>). It also bears mentioning that what little proinflammatory activity is stimulated by <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. is mediated largely by interaction of outer membrane lipoproteins with TLR2 on host cells, and the majority of these proteins are conserved across rough and smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. (<xref rid="B74" ref-type="bibr">Giambartolomei et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B145" ref-type="bibr">Roop et al., 2021</xref>).</p></sec><sec id="S3.SS2"><title>Defense Against Complement and Antimicrobial Peptides</title><p>One of the key functions of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. O-PS is to provide resistance to destruction by complement and antimicrobial peptides (<xref rid="B3" ref-type="bibr">Allen et al., 1998</xref>; <xref rid="B52" ref-type="bibr">Eisenschenk et al., 1999</xref>). Specifically, O-PS has been shown to block access of C1q to outer membrane targets, which results in the known increased susceptibility of rough mutants to complement attack (<xref rid="B118" ref-type="bibr">Moreno et al., 1981</xref>; <xref rid="B37" ref-type="bibr">Corbeil et al., 1988</xref>; <xref rid="B3" ref-type="bibr">Allen et al., 1998</xref>; <xref rid="B52" ref-type="bibr">Eisenschenk et al., 1999</xref>). It is also known that O-PS is important in defense against antimicrobial peptides, including lysozyme, as evidenced by the increased sensitivity of rough mutants to these compounds (<xref rid="B143" ref-type="bibr">Riley and Robertson, 1984</xref>; <xref rid="B107" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;nez de Tejada et al., 1995</xref>; <xref rid="B69" ref-type="bibr">Freer et al., 1996</xref>). Interestingly and in contrast to rough mutants, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> exhibit similar or occasionally greater resistance to complement and antimicrobial peptides compared to their smooth counterparts despite their rough phenotype (<xref rid="B107" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;nez de Tejada et al., 1995</xref>; <xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). In one study, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> was actually found to be the most resistant strain overall to non-immune serum, low pH, H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and cationic peptides (<xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). This indicates that O-PS is not the only factor capable of mediating such peptide resistance in <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>.</p><p>The core oligosaccharide is one such structure involved in complement and bactericidal peptide resistance in both smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. (<xref rid="B160" ref-type="bibr">Soler-Llor&#233;ns et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B66" ref-type="bibr">Fontana et al., 2016</xref>; <xref rid="B148" ref-type="bibr">Salvador-Besc&#243;s et al., 2018</xref>). The role of the core may also help explain an early report that <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> exhibits greater sensitivity to cationic peptides than rough mutants of <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="B70" ref-type="bibr">Freer et al., 1999</xref>). This study utilized <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> REO198, a strain which possesses a core oligosaccharide defect, unlike <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> PA which has an intact core and exhibits enhanced resistance to cationic peptides (<xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>; <xref rid="B130" ref-type="bibr">P&#233;rez-Etayo et al., 2018</xref>). Interestingly, an intact core is also critical for resistance to antimicrobial peptides in other species of Gram-negative bacteria, including <italic toggle="yes">Burkholderia cenocepacia</italic> (<xref rid="B99" ref-type="bibr">Loutet et al., 2006</xref>).</p><p>Yet another feature that contributes to complement resistance in <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. are outer membrane proteins (Omps). As previously mentioned, the pattern of Omp expression differs both between smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and between <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis.</italic> It has been suggested that the differences in Omp expression largely account for the enhanced resistance of natural rough strains against complement and antimicrobial peptides despite the lack of O-PS, in contrast to many rough mutants (<xref rid="B21" ref-type="bibr">Caro-Hern&#225;ndez et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B145" ref-type="bibr">Roop et al., 2021</xref>). These differences may also account for the finding that despite both being naturally rough, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> is highly susceptible to the detergents deoxycholate, Triton X-100, and CHAPS while <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> exhibits comparable or enhanced resistance compared to smooth <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). These findings are important to note as <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, despite both being rough, exhibit notable differences in host preference and zoonotic capability.</p></sec><sec id="S3.SS3"><title>Intracellular Survival and Replication</title><p>The importance of O-PS for smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in survival within their intracellular niche has been well established. O-PS can serve as an adhesin by binding to the scavenger receptor SR-A, allowing interaction with lipid rafts which facilitates entry of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> into the endocytic pathway (<xref rid="B133" ref-type="bibr">Porte et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B93" ref-type="bibr">Kim et al., 2004</xref>) (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>). From there, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> temporarily reside in an endosomal <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>-containing vacuole (eBCV), in which the acidic pH signals the induction of genes encoding the Type-IV secretion system (T4SS). Following secretion of T4SS effectors, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> avoids destruction by lysosomes and traffics to the rough endoplasmic reticulum (RER), where it forms a replicative rBCV in which the bacteria may chronically persist (<xref rid="B27" ref-type="bibr">Celli, 2015</xref>). In contrast, rough mutants cannot enter cells via lipid rafts and vacuoles containing these bacteria fuse rapidly with lysosomes (<xref rid="B133" ref-type="bibr">Porte et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B128" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2008</xref>) (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>). Rough mutants are also internalized more heavily and rapidly (<xref rid="B48" ref-type="bibr">Dornand et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B167" ref-type="bibr">Tian et al., 2014</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, however, more closely resemble the smooth strains in cellular entry and trafficking. For instance, both natural rough strains utilize lipid rafts to enter murine J774.A1 macrophages using the same SR-A receptor, although this interaction is conspicuously not dependent on phosphoinositide-3-kinase (PI3K) as it is with smooth strains (<xref rid="B111" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2010</xref>) (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>).</p><fig id="F3" position="float" orientation="portrait"><label>FIGURE 3</label><caption><p>Cellular entry and intracellular trafficking of natural rough and smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and rough mutants derived from smooth strains. Both natural smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., but not rough mutants, enter macrophages using lipid rafts and utilize the scavenger receptor, SR-A, to do so. While the O-polysaccharide (O-PS) serves as the ligand for this interaction for smooth strains, rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. appear to rely on outer membrane proteins (Omps), including Omp22 and Omp25d. Lipid raft-mediated entry for smooth, but not natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., is reliant on the activity of phosphoinositide-3-kinase (PI3K). Once internalized, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> become engulfed in an endosomal <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>-containing vacuole (eBCV) that alter associates with LAMP1. Expression of the type 4 secretion system (T4SS) allows some of the smooth and natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> to avoid destruction by lysosomes and later associate with components of the rough endoplasmic reticulum (RER), such as calreticulin, to establish a replicative vacuole, or rBCV. In contrast, most rough mutants are unable to avoid lysosomal fusion and do not exhibit intracellular replication. Created with <ext-link xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ext-link-type="uri" xlink:href="http://www.Biorender.com">Biorender.com</ext-link>.</p></caption><graphic xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" position="float" orientation="portrait" xlink:href="fmicb-12-713157-g003.jpg"/></fig><p>Some studies have noted that natural roughs penetrate HeLa cells and murine RAW 264.7 macrophages at a higher rate than smooths, although to a lesser degree than rough mutants (<xref rid="B70" ref-type="bibr">Freer et al., 1999</xref>; <xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>). Other studies have noted that the binding and penetration of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> occurs to a similar extent as smooth strains in murine macrophages and the same observation has been noted for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> in bovine neutrophils and human osteoblast cell lines (<xref rid="B44" ref-type="bibr">Delpino et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B91" ref-type="bibr">Keleher and Skyberg, 2016</xref>). Given the lack of O-PS in both natural and mutant rough strains, it is unclear which molecule(s) may serve as a ligand for SR-A although it is possible that certain Omps may play this role. Two possibilities are Omp25d and Omp22 which are required for entry and replication in murine RAW 264.7 or J774.A1 macrophages by <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> but not by smooth strains in which O-PS predominantly determines cell entry (<xref rid="B106" ref-type="bibr">Manterola et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B108" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2008</xref>).</p><p>Following entry, rough mutants are rapidly destroyed via fusion with lysosomes and are typically unable to replicate intracellularly or exhibit reduced levels of replication within murine J774A.1 macrophages and human monocytes, depending on the mutation (<xref rid="B133" ref-type="bibr">Porte et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B144" ref-type="bibr">Rittig et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B89" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 2004</xref>) (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>). Despite earlier reports showing the inability of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> to do this, it has since been shown that both <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> are fully capable of intracellular replication in macrophages and epithelial cells, with replication of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> noted in HeLa cells, murine RAW 264.7 macrophages, and canine trophoblasts and that for <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> noted in Vero cells, murine J774.A1 macrophages, and HeLa cells (<xref rid="B46" ref-type="bibr">Detilleux et al., 1990</xref>; <xref rid="B108" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B55" ref-type="bibr">Eskra et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B155" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B60" ref-type="bibr">Fern&#225;ndez et al., 2017</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> can replicate in both canine and ovine macrophages and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> exhibits the same ability (<xref rid="B50" ref-type="bibr">Eckstein et al., 2020</xref>). The T4SS appears to be just as important for allowing intracellular replication in the natural roughs as in the smooths as mutants in <italic toggle="yes">virB</italic> genes or <italic toggle="yes">vjbR</italic> of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> exhibit significant attenuation and inability to replicate within ovine macrophages and mice or HeLa cells, murine RAW 264.7 macrophages, and canine DH82 dendritic cells, respectively (<xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B100" ref-type="bibr">Macedo et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B162" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2020</xref>).</p><p>Interestingly, while phagosomes containing rough mutants fuse rapidly with lysosomes and many are unable to progress beyond this point to establish replication in the rBCV, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> appear to eventually be able to reach the replicative niche and replicate to levels identical to smooth strains in HeLa cells and murine RAW264.7 macrophages (<xref rid="B155" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>) (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F3">Figure 3</xref>). <xref rid="B155" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2014</xref> demonstrated that BCVs containing <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in HeLa cells interact early in infection with lysosomes, as shown by association with LAMP1, and that this marker is later excluded with formation of the typical rBCV associated with calreticulin, a marker of the RER. Formation of myelin figures associated with vacuole-enclosed bacteria in ovine macrophages also suggests that <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> may continue in the pathway followed by smooth strains with formation of an autophagic vacuole for cellular egress (<xref rid="B100" ref-type="bibr">Macedo et al., 2015</xref>). Interestingly, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> also exhibits high levels of lysosome fusion in the first few hours of cellular infection, comparable to rough mutants and significantly less than smooth strains, but is able to replicate after 48 h comparable to <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> levels in HeLa cells and murine J774.A1 or RAW264.7 macrophages, (<xref rid="B133" ref-type="bibr">Porte et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>). Nevertheless, differences have been observed: <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> shows later evasion of lysosome fusion compared to smooth strains, with this change happening after 48 h in ovine macrophages instead of the 24 h noted for <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> in murine J774.A1 macrophages (<xref rid="B161" ref-type="bibr">Starr et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B155" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2014</xref>). For <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, a similar trend has been noted in which colocalization of the bacterium with calnexin, another RER marker, does not occur in HeLa cells until 48 h post-infection (<xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>). This delay in avoidance of phagosome-lysosome fusion may help explain why earlier studies, which did not extend time points beyond 24 h, demonstrated prominent and rapid phagosome fusion with lysosomes using natural rough strains in contrast to smooth strains, with the conclusion that natural roughs could not replicate intracellularly (<xref rid="B133" ref-type="bibr">Porte et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B144" ref-type="bibr">Rittig et al., 2003</xref>). It has been well established that natural rough <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> can replicate within cells both from their natural hosts as well as mice and humans.</p></sec><sec id="S3.SS4"><title>Prevention of Apoptosis and Cellular Activation</title><p>O-PS-mediated entry by smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. via lipid rafts has been shown to inhibit caspase-2-mediated apoptosis in phagocytes, including murine J774.A1 and RAW264.7 macrophages and human macrophages (<xref rid="B61" ref-type="bibr">Fernandez-Prada et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B129" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B29" ref-type="bibr">Chen and He, 2009</xref>). Rough mutants, on their other hand, are well known for induction of cytotoxicity which is dependent on the T4SS in the same cell types (<xref rid="B61" ref-type="bibr">Fernandez-Prada et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B128" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2008</xref>). While the mechanisms behind this are incompletely understood, it appears that overexpression of the T4SS occurs during cellular infection by rough mutants, leading to over-secretion of effector proteins which activate the IRE1&#945; pathway of ER stress (<xref rid="B128" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B97" ref-type="bibr">Li et al., 2017</xref>). Interestingly, this seems not to be the case with natural rough strains, where <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> resemble their smooth counterparts and have been repeatedly shown not to induce cytotoxicity in HeLa cells, murine J774.A1 and RAW 264.7 macrophages, or human lung epithelial cells (<xref rid="B108" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B63" ref-type="bibr">Ferrero et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>).</p><p>O-PS-mediated uptake of smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. is also known to induce only low levels of proinflammatory cytokines in phagocytes, especially in comparison to other Gram-negative pathogens (<xref rid="B89" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 2004</xref>; <xref rid="B12" ref-type="bibr">Billard et al., 2007</xref>). This is an attribute which has been added to the list of ways in which <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. can subvert the host immune response. Nevertheless, it is important to note that despite this low induction of inflammatory cytokines, inflammation is still a classic feature of brucellosis and although typically milder compared to that caused by most other bacteria, the chronic stimulation of low-level inflammation by persisting <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. frequently results in tissue damage (<xref rid="B7" ref-type="bibr">Baldi and Giambartolomei, 2013</xref>). In contrast to smooth strains, penetration by rough mutants leads to significant production of proinflammatory cytokines in human monocytes or macrophages and murine J774.A1 macrophages (<xref rid="B61" ref-type="bibr">Fernandez-Prada et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B144" ref-type="bibr">Rittig et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B128" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2008</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, like the smooth strains, also induce minimal to no proinflammatory cytokine production in cells while rough mutants, including RB51, stimulate high levels of TNF-&#945; and IL-12 in murine J774.A1 macrophages, although this pattern has yet to be investigated in macrophages derived from their natural hosts (<xref rid="B111" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2010</xref>). A possible explanation for this is that, like the smooth strains, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> enter cells via lipid rafts and follow the same general intracellular trafficking pathway, albeit with some delay, while the contrasting entry by rough mutants triggers a strong cellular response. The differences in the intracellular pathway between natural rough and smooth strains, such as the ligand for SR-A and timing of lysosome evasion, have been proposed to account for the further reduced inflammatory response of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> when compared with smooth strains in mice and natural hosts (<xref rid="B71" ref-type="bibr">Galindo et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B145" ref-type="bibr">Roop et al., 2021</xref>).</p><p>While it is apparent that O-PS serves directly or indirectly as a key virulence factor for smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., the lack of this molecule in natural rough strains appears to be compensated for, at least partially, by alterations in Omp expression and the continued presence of the lateral core oligosaccharide side branch (<xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B145" ref-type="bibr">Roop et al., 2021</xref>). The possibility that a rough phenotype in itself could serve an additional advantage to <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. is intriguing and this idea can best be contemplated after understanding the reasons why other Gram-negative pathogens, such as <italic toggle="yes">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">Yersinia pestis</italic>, have either temporarily or permanently lost their O-PS.</p></sec></sec><sec id="S4"><title>The Advantages of Going Rough</title><p>Naturally smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. can undergo temporary or permanent loss of O-PS under both laboratory conditions and during the course of infection within their natural hosts (<xref rid="B103" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla, 2015</xref>). This process is exemplified by RB51, a spontaneous and stably rough vaccine strain that arose after repeated passage on antibiotic-containing media and has been widely utilized in studies investigating the function of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> O-PS (<xref rid="B150" ref-type="bibr">Schurig et al., 1991</xref>). Another example is the now out of use <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> 45/20 vaccine strain, which acquired a rough phenotype after repeated passage through guinea pigs (<xref rid="B120" ref-type="bibr">Moriy&#243;n et al., 2004</xref>).</p><p>Dissociation from a smooth to a rough phenotype and the potential advantages of this change for the bacterium have been studied for many years with various Gram-negative bacteria. <xref rid="B141" ref-type="bibr">Reeves (1995)</xref> suggested that the tendency of Gram-negative bacteria to become rough under laboratory conditions is due to the necessity of O-PS for survival only in the presence of the host immune response. Requiring energy to produce, there appears to be a selective advantage to loss of O-PS in situations where it is not needed, as in liquid culture (<xref rid="B101" ref-type="bibr">Maldonado et al., 2016</xref>). Isolation of rough variants of smooth Gram-negative bacteria in samples from infected hosts, including <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>, also suggests that there may be an adaptive advantage to loss of O-PS in evading the host immune system, particularly in chronic infections (<xref rid="B171" ref-type="bibr">Turse et al., 2011</xref>; <xref rid="B105" ref-type="bibr">Mancilla et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B166" ref-type="bibr">Szabo et al., 2017</xref>). Although this seems to contradict the statement that Gram-negative bacteria require O-PS to resist destruction by the host immune system, namely via complement, it appears that various bacteria will sacrifice the protection offered by a complete O-PS in exchange for additional survival benefits such as avoidance of a robust antibody response and improved access to preferred host-cell types (<xref rid="B101" ref-type="bibr">Maldonado et al., 2016</xref>). Could it be that a rough phenotype in <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> or <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> is not only compensated for by changes to the outer membrane but may actually benefit these organisms by assisting in maintenance of a chronic infection? While the answer to this question remains uncertain, several other Gram-negative bacteria develop a rough phenotype during chronic infections.</p><p>One example is <italic toggle="yes">P. aeruginosa</italic>, particularly in patients with cystic fibrosis (CF). While isolates from acutely infected patients or the environment typically have a smooth phenotype, most from chronically infected patients with CF are rough (<xref rid="B82" ref-type="bibr">Hancock et al., 1983</xref>; <xref rid="B78" ref-type="bibr">Goldberg and Pler, 1996</xref>; <xref rid="B95" ref-type="bibr">Lam et al., 2011</xref>). The reasons behind this phenomenon and its propensity for occurring in CF patients are not fully understood, but it has been suggested that while rough <italic toggle="yes">P. aeruginosa</italic> strains are more serum-sensitive, the reduced immunostimulatory potential following loss of the O-PS may contribute to immune evasion and survival in chronic lung infections. Like their smooth counterparts, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> can avoid activation of macrophages along with subsequent proinflammatory cytokine production (<xref rid="B111" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2010</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> in particular has been shown to be less inflammatory in the mouse model in terms of pathologic changes and cytokine production and induces less ROS production in infected humans than smooth strains despite similarly low levels of cytokine induction noted by the previously mentioned <italic toggle="yes">in vitro</italic> studies (<xref rid="B173" ref-type="bibr">Usta et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). As suggested by <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al. (2015)</xref>, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> might therefore be an even stealthier pathogen in its natural host than smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., although this possibility remains to be tested in dogs or canine cells. The rough phenotype of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> could possibly serve an advantage by reducing immune stimulation, allowing it to establish long-lasting, asymptomatic infections, while simultaneously avoiding the serum sensitivity observed with other rough Gram-negative bacteria via adjustments to the structure of its outer membrane.</p><p>Another example of a bacterium that benefits from loss of O-PS is <italic toggle="yes">B. cenocepacia</italic>, which also undergoes this change during chronic infection of CF patients (<xref rid="B58" ref-type="bibr">Evans et al., 1999</xref>). Assumption of a rough phenotype facilitates internalization into macrophages, a niche that this facultatively intracellular pathogen could subsequently exploit to favor persistence (<xref rid="B147" ref-type="bibr">Sald&#237;as et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B151" ref-type="bibr">Schwab et al., 2014</xref>). Enhanced phagocytosis by macrophages is also observed in both rough mutants and, depending on the study, with the naturally rough <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> (<xref rid="B46" ref-type="bibr">Detilleux et al., 1990</xref>; <xref rid="B167" ref-type="bibr">Tian et al., 2014</xref>). Whether this enhanced entry could also assist rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in establishing their preferred intracellular niche early in infection is unknown. It also bears mentioning that enhanced entry of natural and mutant rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. has been noted in various cell types beyond macrophages, including epithelial and endothelial cells and without associated replication (<xref rid="B63" ref-type="bibr">Ferrero et al., 2009</xref>, <xref rid="B62" ref-type="bibr">2011</xref>).</p><p>The ability to exploit new host cell receptors by exhibiting a rough phenotype might serve a third possible advantage for Gram-negative bacteria. For instance, the normally smooth <italic toggle="yes">Helicobacter pylori</italic> is able to bind to a receptor called TFF1 on injured gastric mucosa specifically through rough LPS, as demonstrated by work examining the interaction of TFF1 with purified <italic toggle="yes">H. pylori</italic> rough LPS or a rough mutant (<xref rid="B140" ref-type="bibr">Reeves et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B47" ref-type="bibr">Dolan et al., 2012</xref>). Both naturally rough and smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. utilize the scavenger receptor, SR-A (CD36) to enter macrophages (<xref rid="B128" ref-type="bibr">Pei et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B111" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2010</xref>). Whether the exposure of the core and additional outer membrane components such as Omps could allow naturally rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to exploit additional receptors unavailable to smooth strains in their natural hosts is uncertain. That this may be the case is supported by the finding that O-PS may interfere with interaction of Omps with anti-<italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> antibodies (<xref rid="B15" ref-type="bibr">Bowden et al., 1995</xref>).</p><p>Another Gram-negative bacterium deserves special mention here: <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic>. Like <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic> has permanently lost its O-PS yet remains a virulent pathogen. Although the structure of its LPS differs significantly from that of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> (<xref ref-type="fig" rid="F1">Figure 1</xref>), <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic> exhibits several similarities in its behavior such as the ability to survive in macrophage phagosomes and inhibition of fusion with lysosomes to allow for intracellular replication (<xref rid="B134" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B36" ref-type="bibr">Connor et al., 2018</xref>). The rough LPS provides a selective advantage to this pathogen by enabling exposure of the protein Ail at the cell surface, resulting in thickening and rigidification of the LPS which actually promotes serum resistance (<xref rid="B157" ref-type="bibr">Singh et al., 2020</xref>). This is reminiscent of the high serum resistance of the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> but the exact structural modifications resulting in this enhanced resistance for <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> have not been fully unraveled (<xref rid="B21" ref-type="bibr">Caro-Hern&#225;ndez et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). Additionally, the rough <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic> is able to use its exposed core to bind SIGNR1 (CD209b), a receptor on antigen-presenting cells, facilitating its dissemination to various organs (<xref rid="B182" ref-type="bibr">Yang et al., 2019</xref>). Interestingly, experimentally derived smooth <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic> actually has an impaired ability to cause systemic infection in mice, leading to the proposal that loss of O-PS was key to the evolution of <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic> as a highly virulent pathogen (<xref rid="B182" ref-type="bibr">Yang et al., 2019</xref>). As described above, the absence of O-PS in <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> coincides with an altered topology of the outer membrane, particularly in regards to its Omps, which also serves to increase serum resistance (<xref rid="B21" ref-type="bibr">Caro-Hern&#225;ndez et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B110" ref-type="bibr">Mart&#237;n-Mart&#237;n et al., 2011</xref>). The ability of rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to exploit additional receptors, again, is unknown but is an intriguing possibility which might also explain their host specificity and organ tropism.</p><p>It seems clear that natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. have compensated for their lack of O-PS, as evidenced by their resistance to complement degradation, ability to replicate intracellularly, and lack of cytotoxicity and induction of proinflammatory cytokines. As with other Gram-negative bacteria, the rough phenotype might also serve to enhance the ability of rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to avoid immune detection. Whether a rough LPS might also serve an advantage by increasing entry into macrophages or allowing the bacterium to use different receptors for cellular entry is not known but should be further explored, particularly in the context of the natural hosts.</p></sec><sec id="S5"><title>Smooth and Rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>: Interaction With the Host Immune System</title><p>Although some differences do exist, interaction of natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. with host cells, including cellular entry, replication, and lack of proinflammatory cytokine production, closely resembles what occurs with smooth strains. These numerous similarities in host cell interactions suggest that components of the immune response engendered by both smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. may be similar, a concept which has significant implications for vaccine development. Work with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in cells derived from the natural host and in the mouse model, described in the following section, may help to address this question.</p><sec id="S5.SS1"><title>MHC-II Modulation</title><p>One mechanism in which smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. interact with host cells to subvert the adaptive immune response is by inhibition of antigen presentation via Major Histocompatibility Complex (MHC)-II in macrophages. Macrophages utilize MHC-II to present antigen to CD4+ T lymphocytes, stimulating the adaptive immune response (<xref rid="B42" ref-type="bibr">Cresswell, 1994</xref>). The ability of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to interfere with this process is crucial to its long-term survival within the host, as IFN-&#947;-producing CD4+ T lymphocytes are a well-recognized requirement to controlling <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> infection (<xref rid="B174" ref-type="bibr">Vitry et al., 2012</xref>). Interestingly, <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> O-PS has also been found to interfere with this crucial branch between innate and adaptive immunity. One mechanism by which this occurs is disrupting the ability of MHC-II to present antigen following processing of LPS by macrophages. In this scenario, smooth LPS shed within the BCV is degraded by the macrophage followed by the formation of dense complexes, or macrodomains, which interfere with the interaction of MHC-II with CD4+ T lymphocytes through steric hindrance (<xref rid="B68" ref-type="bibr">Forestier et al., 1999</xref>, <xref rid="B67" ref-type="bibr">2000</xref>). However, it appears that an additional mechanism for MHC modulation exists that is utilized by the natural rough strains as well. In a study using THP-1 cells, <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic>, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, and heat-killed <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> inhibited IFN-&#947;-induced expression of MHC-II, suggesting the importance of some conserved element (<xref rid="B10" ref-type="bibr">Barrionuevo et al., 2008</xref>). Rather than LPS, Omp19 was the molecule responsible for this inhibition and this occurred in a TLR2-dependent manner (<xref rid="B10" ref-type="bibr">Barrionuevo et al., 2008</xref>). Thus, natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> are capable of decreasing antigen presentation via MHC-II during the development of an adaptive immune response, independent of the structure of their LPS. Like the smooth strains, natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. can use MHC-II modulation to avoid detection by CD4+ T lymphocytes, promoting the chronic infections observed in their natural hosts.</p></sec><sec id="S5.SS2"><title>Interaction With Natural Host Cells</title><p>As professional antigen-presenting cells, dendritic cells play a key role in linking innate and adaptive immunity, and their role in the immune response to <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> has been extensively evaluated. In terms of smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic>, there remains some controversy as to whether infection of dendritic cells stimulates or inhibits their activation (<xref rid="B6" ref-type="bibr">Avila-Calder&#243;n et al., 2020</xref>). Overall, the majority of studies have shown that smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. infection of dendritic cells results in downregulation of costimulatory markers (i.e., CD80 and CD86) and decreased production of proinflammatory cytokines while infection with rough mutants, such as RB51, results in the opposite effect (<xref rid="B184" ref-type="bibr">Zwerdling et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B165" ref-type="bibr">Surendran et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B6" ref-type="bibr">Avila-Calder&#243;n et al., 2020</xref>). The core oligosaccharide side branch, mentioned before for its importance in defense against complement and hampering of TLR4 recognition, appears to be important here as well. The <italic toggle="yes">wadC</italic> mutants of smooth strains, lacking this side branch, induce significant dendritic cell activation through recognition by TLR4 while their smooth parent strains do not (<xref rid="B35" ref-type="bibr">Conde-&#193;lvarez et al., 2012</xref>). Comparatively little work on interaction with dendritic cells has been done with natural rough strains, but two studies by <xref rid="B136" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al. (2017)</xref> have produced interesting findings concerning <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. These authors demonstrated that <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> induces the expression of costimulatory molecules on both canine and human dendritic cells, but while infected human dendritic cells demonstrate TH1-skewed proinflammatory cytokine production, the canine cells show a mixed TH1-TH17 response (<xref rid="B136" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al., 2017</xref>). This effect was also observed in CD4+ T lymphocytes stimulated by dendritic cells previously infected with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> (<xref rid="B135" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al., 2019</xref>). These findings demonstrate that host specificity can play an important role in the immune response to <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp.</p><p>A strong TH1 response, required for effective control of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> infection, by human dendritic cells could at least partially account for the lower susceptibility of humans to <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> infection compared to the natural canine host. TH17 cells, on the other hand, have been shown to be associated with development of osteoarticular lesions in mice infected with <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., with IL-17 driving osteoclastogenesis (<xref rid="B73" ref-type="bibr">Giambartolomei et al., 2012</xref>). TH17 lymphocytes are also associated with autoimmune disorders and auto-antibody formation (<xref rid="B39" ref-type="bibr">Cornelius and Lamarca, 2014</xref>). The TH17 component of the response in canine dendritic cells, as suggested by <xref rid="B136" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al. (2017)</xref>, may reflect an increased susceptibility in this host and/or help to explain the incidence of osteoarticular lesions, particularly diskospondylitis, as well as antibody-mediated destruction of sperm in infected dogs (<xref rid="B72" ref-type="bibr">George and Carmichael, 1984</xref>; <xref rid="B135" ref-type="bibr">Pujol et al., 2019</xref>). Antibody-mediated attack on sperm is also a prominent feature of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> infection in rams and the possible role of IL-17 remains to be explored in this species (<xref rid="B124" ref-type="bibr">Paolicchi et al., 2000</xref>). The association with IL-17 and disease in dogs infected with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> also warrants investigation. In addition to potential detrimental effects of IL-17 during <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> infection, this cytokine appears to play some role in protective immunity early-on in the lungs following mucosal exposure (<xref rid="B102" ref-type="bibr">Mambres et al., 2016</xref>). The effect of IL-17 on defense against <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., smooth and rough, therefore appears to differ depending on the site of infection and deserves further study.</p><p>An additional host cell type that has been explored with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> infection is canine trophoblasts. <xref rid="B60" ref-type="bibr">Fern&#225;ndez et al. (2017)</xref> found that <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> can infect and replicate within these cells or canine placental explants, causing no cytotoxicity but resulting in the secretion of IL-8 and RANTES (CCL5). Similar stimulation of IL-8 production has been seen in bovine placental explants, human trophoblasts, and human endometrial cell lines infected with <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> (<xref rid="B22" ref-type="bibr">Carvalho Neta et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B59" ref-type="bibr">Fern&#225;ndez et al., 2016</xref>; <xref rid="B183" ref-type="bibr">Zavattieri et al., 2020</xref>). When the canine or human trophoblasts were exposed to culture supernatant from phagocytes infected with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> or <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic>, the same stimulation of IL-8 was observed, suspected to be due to production of TNF-&#945; by the phagocytes as has been shown to be the case in humans (<xref rid="B152" ref-type="bibr">Shimoya et al., 1999</xref>; <xref rid="B59" ref-type="bibr">Fern&#225;ndez et al., 2016</xref>, <xref rid="B60" ref-type="bibr">2017</xref>). Increased IL-8 is expected to be responsible for drawing in neutrophils seen in the necrotizing placentitis characteristic in cases of infection with both <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> in dogs or smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in their natural hosts (<xref rid="B60" ref-type="bibr">Fern&#225;ndez et al., 2017</xref>). Thus, it appears that both rough <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and the virulent smooth strains exhibit a similar pathogenesis in the female reproductive system in a still incompletely defined mechanism that is independent of O-PS. It remains to be seen how this cytokine induction compares with <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, which is predominantly a pathogen of the male reproductive system with abortion being less common.</p></sec><sec id="S5.SS3"><title>Infection in the Mouse Model</title><p>Unlike many rough mutants, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> are able to establish and maintain a chronic, systemic infection in mice with organ distribution typical of smooth strains (<xref rid="B88" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1993</xref>; <xref rid="B156" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2011</xref>; <xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). However, the dose required to do so appears to be higher, at least for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. For infection of mice with smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., a dose of 10<sup>4</sup>&#8211;10<sup>5</sup> CFU is typically used, resulting in early colonization of the spleen and liver with persistence in the spleen typically lasting longer than 36 weeks (<xref rid="B84" ref-type="bibr">High et al., 2007</xref>; <xref rid="B80" ref-type="bibr">Grill&#243; et al., 2012</xref>). However, infection with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> at this dose in a previous study resulted in sporadic levels of colonization in the spleen with clearance achieved by 9 weeks (<xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>).
