Vaccine studies in mice have noted levels of colonization in the spleen similar to or higher than the inoculation dose of 5 &#215; 10<sup>4</sup>&#8211;5 &#215; 10<sup>5</sup>, although these studies only examined a single time point post-infection and the full picture of the course of infection is difficult to ascertain in these cases (<xref rid="B51" ref-type="bibr">Edmonds et al., 2002</xref>; <xref rid="B31" ref-type="bibr">Clausse et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B137" ref-type="bibr">Qian et al., 2017</xref>). Regardless, what is clear is that both <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> appear to be less proinflammatory in mice than their smooth counterparts although it must be noted that in their natural hosts, both natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. can induce significant inflammation in target organs, including the epididymis (<xref rid="B20" ref-type="bibr">Carmichael and Kenney, 1968</xref>; <xref rid="B64" ref-type="bibr">Ficapal et al., 1998</xref>).</p><p>Splenomegaly is a classic feature of smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. infection in mice (<xref rid="B53" ref-type="bibr">Enright et al., 1990</xref>; <xref rid="B80" ref-type="bibr">Grill&#243; et al., 2012</xref>). In contrast, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> at doses up to 10<sup>7</sup> CFU does not result in significant splenomegaly (<xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). This gross lesion may be induced at a high dose of 10<sup>9</sup> CFU, but even then, the effect is transient (<xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). Interestingly, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> does consistently appear to result in splenomegaly (<xref rid="B156" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2011</xref>). <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> also produces less significant histologic lesions in target organs, including fewer microgranulomas in the liver and histiocytic infiltrates in the spleen, than smooth strains despite identical inoculation doses and levels of colonization (<xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). Unexpectedly, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> is able to induce numerous microgranulomas in the liver at 10<sup>6</sup> CFU while such lesions are scarce in mice infected with the same dose of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, emphasizing that while both rough, <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> are by no means identical (<xref rid="B156" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2011</xref>; <xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B163" ref-type="bibr">Stranahan et al., 2019</xref>). In terms of cytokine induction <italic toggle="yes">in vivo</italic>, smooth strains are known to not cause significant induction of proinflammatory cytokines in mice (<xref rid="B9" ref-type="bibr">Barquero-Calvo et al., 2007</xref>). Nevertheless, both <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> produce even lower amounts of such cytokines, including IFN-&#947; and IL-6, than smooth strains at the same dose (<xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>). This is in contrast to the high levels of proinflammatory cytokines induced <italic toggle="yes">in vitro</italic> by rough mutants and it has been suggested, as mentioned above, that the lower levels induced by <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> indicate that these strains exhibit an even stealthier intracellular life style (<xref rid="B144" ref-type="bibr">Rittig et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B146" ref-type="bibr">S&#225; et al., 2012</xref>; <xref rid="B28" ref-type="bibr">Chac&#243;n-D&#237;az et al., 2015</xref>).</p><p>The distribution of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> to the spleen, liver, and lymph nodes in mice, as in their natural hosts, indicates that this laboratory animal can serve as a model for vaccine efficacy studies as it does for smooth strains (<xref rid="B20" ref-type="bibr">Carmichael and Kenney, 1968</xref>; <xref rid="B153" ref-type="bibr">Silva A. P. et al., 2015</xref>). Nevertheless, there are some limitations. For <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>, a significant cause of epididymitis in rams, one such deficiency is the lack of genital tropism or significant histologic lesions induced in the male reproductive system of mice (<xref rid="B156" ref-type="bibr">Silva et al., 2011</xref>). While <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> is able to colonize the non-pregnant uterus of mice, it remains to be seen for both <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> whether these natural rough strains can induce placentitis and fetal resorption noted with smooth strain infection in mice (<xref rid="B80" ref-type="bibr">Grill&#243; et al., 2012</xref>).</p><p>The mouse model has been frequently employed to test vaccine candidates for <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and less commonly for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. For smooth strains, mice have been heavily utilized to investigate correlates of immune protection against infection, particularly in the context of vaccination. Such studies have established that control of smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. infection is reliant on a strong TH1 immune response with IFN-&#947; representing the critical cytokine involved (<xref rid="B121" ref-type="bibr">Murphy et al., 2001</xref>; <xref rid="B8" ref-type="bibr">Baldwin and Goenka, 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B158" ref-type="bibr">Skendros and Boura, 2013</xref>). CD4+ and CD8+ T lymphocytes are the primary producers of IFN-&#947; in brucellosis and both appear to play key roles in the protective immune response, although the relative importance of each subset compared to the other remains controversial and appears to be influenced by route of infection and strain administered (<xref rid="B102" ref-type="bibr">Mambres et al., 2016</xref>; <xref rid="B127" ref-type="bibr">Pascual et al., 2019</xref>; <xref rid="B176" ref-type="bibr">Wang et al., 2020</xref>). For example, both CD4+ T lymphocytes and B lymphocytes are required to control infection with <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> in mice following i.p. inoculation while only &#945;/&#946; T lymphocytes (either CD4+ or CD8+) are required for control following intranasal infection (<xref rid="B102" ref-type="bibr">Mambres et al., 2016</xref>; <xref rid="B45" ref-type="bibr">Demars et al., 2019</xref>). Comparatively little has been done to investigate the components of a protective immune response against the natural rough strains, particularly for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. Nevertheless, protection against colonization induced by vaccination against both <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> has been correlated with increased levels of IFN-&#947; production in mice (<xref rid="B25" ref-type="bibr">Cassataro et al., 2007b</xref>; <xref rid="B31" ref-type="bibr">Clausse et al., 2014</xref>; <xref rid="B50" ref-type="bibr">Eckstein et al., 2020</xref>; <xref rid="B116" ref-type="bibr">Moran et al., 2021</xref>). Additionally, as has been shown with <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic>, antibodies appear to play an important role in protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in mice (<xref rid="B87" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1994</xref>; <xref rid="B15" ref-type="bibr">Bowden et al., 1995</xref>). Further investigation is required with the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. to determine additional correlates of immune protection.</p></sec></sec><sec id="S6"><title>Vaccine Development for Rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp.</title><p>While the vaccines <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> S19, <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> RB51, and <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> Rev.1 are commercially available to protect against infection by smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. in livestock, no such vaccines exist for use in dogs. In addition, the Rev.1 vaccine, although protective against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in sheep, is not approved for use in areas free of <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> (<xref rid="B14" ref-type="bibr">Blasco and D&#237;az, 1993</xref>; <xref rid="B142" ref-type="bibr">Ridler and West, 2011</xref>). Three questions may be asked in terms of the much-needed vaccine development for rough <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>: (1) Is a live attenuated or killed/subunit vaccine superior? (2) Is the immune response required to protect against natural roughs by vaccination the same as that required against smooth strains? (3) Does it matter if a live attenuated vaccine (LAV) to protect against the natural roughs is smooth or rough and will this impact cross-protection against smooth strains of <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> in the vaccinated animal? The first question is still debated even for the smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., although a preponderance of evidence from the natural host indicates that LAVs provide the greatest levels of protection against brucellosis due to their ability to generate more persistent memory responses (<xref rid="B65" ref-type="bibr">Ficht et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B131" ref-type="bibr">Perkins et al., 2010</xref>). When comparing the protection indices of studies investigating vaccine candidates against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, as measured by differences in log<sub>10</sub> colonization of the spleen between vaccinated and unvaccinated animals, the picture is less clear (<xref rid="T2" ref-type="table">Tables 2</xref>, <xref rid="T3" ref-type="table">3</xref>). Higher protection indices are typically noted for LAVs than for subunit vaccines, although there is frequent overlap and many instances in which protection afforded by subunit vaccines approaches or equals that offered by LAVs. Work with <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> has highlighted that the choice of adjuvant can significantly impact the efficacy of a subunit vaccine (<xref rid="B31" ref-type="bibr">Clausse et al., 2014</xref>) and studies involving both natural rough strains have demonstrated improved protection of certain LAVs when given in an encapsulated form, as has been shown for smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. (<xref rid="B65" ref-type="bibr">Ficht et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B153" ref-type="bibr">Silva A. P. et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B50" ref-type="bibr">Eckstein et al., 2020</xref>).</p><table-wrap id="T2" position="float" orientation="portrait"><label>TABLE 2</label><caption><p>Vaccine candidates against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> investigated in the mouse model in select studies.</p></caption><table frame="hsides" rules="groups" cellspacing="5" cellpadding="5"><thead><tr><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Candidate vaccine</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Route/dose</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Mouse strain</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Challenge strain, dose/route</td><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1"># vaccinations</td><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Protection index</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">References</td></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>&#916;<italic toggle="yes">abcBA</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">10<sup>8</sup> CFU, s.q., alginate-encapsulated</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">BALB/c</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> ATCC 25840, 10<sup>6</sup> CFU, i.p.</td><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1">1</td><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1">0.54</td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><xref rid="B154" ref-type="bibr">Silva T. valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1"><italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic></td><td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">10<sup>9</sup> CFU, i.p.</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1">1</td><td valign="top" align="center" rowspan="1" colspan="1">4.12</td><td rowspan="1" colspan="1"/></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><attrib><italic toggle="yes">White cells indicate studies involving live attenuated vaccine (LAV) candidates while gray cells indicate those associated with subunit/killed candidates.</italic></attrib></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap><p>As for the components of a protective immune response against rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., what is so far known from research in mice, as indicated in the preceding section, appears to reflect what is observed for smooth strains. In the few vaccine studies which have examined it, IFN-&#947; is higher in stimulated splenocytes in groups of mice that exhibit greater levels of protection against both <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> (<xref rid="B26" ref-type="bibr">Cassataro et al., 2005b</xref>, <xref rid="B25" ref-type="bibr">2007b</xref>; <xref rid="B168" ref-type="bibr">Truong et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B50" ref-type="bibr">Eckstein et al., 2020</xref>; <xref rid="B116" ref-type="bibr">Moran et al., 2021</xref>). There is also limited evidence that antibodies play a role in protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in vaccinated mice (<xref rid="B87" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1994</xref>). For the natural dog and ovine hosts, a TH1 response also appears critical for protection. In dogs, the current understanding is that animals which achieve self-elimination of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, at least to below the level of detection by culture, are immune to subsequent challenge secondary to a strong cell-mediated immune response (<xref rid="B19" ref-type="bibr">Carmichael and Greene, 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B40" ref-type="bibr">Cosford, 2018</xref>). In contrast, persistently infected dogs exhibit a stronger humoral immune response and these dogs that undergo treatment with antibiotics are fully susceptible to secondary challenge (<xref rid="B19" ref-type="bibr">Carmichael and Greene, 2006</xref>). For sheep, the relative importance of humoral immunity is less certain but TH1/cell-mediated responses with high levels of IFN-&#947; are associated with higher levels of protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in Rev.1- or subunit-vaccinated animals (<xref rid="B57" ref-type="bibr">Estein et al., 2009</xref>; <xref rid="B71" ref-type="bibr">Galindo et al., 2009</xref>).</p><p>While CD4+ T lymphocytes were long considered the most important source of IFN-&#947; in the response against brucellosis, several recent studies have indicated that CD8+ T lymphocytes may be equally or sometimes more important in mediating protection depending on the vaccine administered. For example, one study found that CD4+ T lymphocytes were required for protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> in mice following vaccination with RB51 while CD8+ T lymphocytes were required following vaccination with the candidate <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus znBAZ</italic> (<xref rid="B176" ref-type="bibr">Wang et al., 2020</xref>). Whether this is related to the rough phenotype of RB51 or separate properties of this LAV is uncertain. For the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., such aspects of the immune response during vaccination have been little explored but also appear to be influenced by the type of vaccine. In work investigating the protective efficacy of Omp31 as a vaccine against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in mice, protection afforded by administration of the recombinant protein was mediated by CD4+ T lymphocytes while protection acquired through DNA coding for Omp31 was mediated mainly by CD8+ T lymphocytes (<xref rid="B23" ref-type="bibr">Cassataro et al., 2005a</xref>, <xref rid="B26" ref-type="bibr">b</xref>). It is clear that further work needs to be done to investigate components of a protective immune response against natural rough strains induced by vaccination, particularly for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. An additional need is to perform these investigations using a natural, mucosal route of infection rather than the convenient but artificial intraperitoneal (i.p.) route. This concern has become apparent recently as studies with smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. indicate that correlates of immune protection differ depending on route of infection, with B cells, for instance, being required for protection in mice following i.p. inoculation but being dispensable following intranasal infection (<xref rid="B102" ref-type="bibr">Mambres et al., 2016</xref>; <xref rid="B45" ref-type="bibr">Demars et al., 2019</xref>).</p><p>Finally, should a vaccine against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> or <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> be produced in a smooth or rough background and will this impact cross-protection against smooth strains? This question is critical as both sheep and dogs are susceptible to infection by smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., including <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> for sheep and <italic toggle="yes">B. suis</italic> for dogs (<xref rid="B126" ref-type="bibr">Pappas et al., 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B139" ref-type="bibr">Ramamoorthy et al., 2011</xref>). Unlike the vaccines S19 and Rev.1, RB51 is a stable rough vaccine. While its LPS phenotype putatively results in less interference with serologic assays, RB51 possesses the disadvantages of antibiotic resistance, virulence for humans, and decreased efficacy compared to S19 (<xref rid="B181" ref-type="bibr">Winter et al., 1996</xref>; <xref rid="B65" ref-type="bibr">Ficht et al., 2009</xref>). Interestingly, while RB51 provides protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in mice, it is not likewise effective in sheep (<xref rid="B87" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1994</xref>, <xref rid="B86" ref-type="bibr">1995</xref>). Of note, this study suggested through passive transfer that the protection afforded against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in mice was predominantly mediated by the humoral immune response while protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> was mainly cell-mediated, although this protection was much lower than that afforded by the vaccine (<xref rid="B87" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1994</xref>). RB51 is also able to provide similar levels of protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. abortus</italic> in mice (<xref rid="B168" ref-type="bibr">Truong et al., 2015</xref>). Efficacy of RB51 in dogs is uncertain and although it is not shed in the urine or feces of vaccinated dogs, it can colonize various organs, including the placenta, which could serve as a source of infection to humans or other animals and therefore precludes its use in dogs (<xref rid="B122" ref-type="bibr">Palmer and Cheville, 1997</xref>).</p><p>The deficiencies of RB51 do not mean that rough vaccines cannot be protective against infection by <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., rough or smooth. RB51&#8217;s reduced efficacy against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and its smooth counterparts in the natural host may be associated with its attenuation and/or alterations to its outer membrane or LPS outside of its lack of O-PS. Several studies have demonstrated that targeted mutagenesis can produce rough vaccines that are superior in protection to RB51 and comparable to protection by S19 or Rev.1 in mice (<xref rid="B114" ref-type="bibr">Monreal et al., 2003</xref><xref rid="B90" ref-type="bibr">Kahl-McDonagh and Ficht, 2006</xref>; <xref rid="B5" ref-type="bibr">Arag&#243;n-Aranda et al., 2020</xref>). One prominent finding is that for a rough vaccine to be effective, the core must be intact although lack of the aforementioned lateral side branch may actually enhance vaccine efficacy, presumably due to enhanced recognition of surface molecules in the absence of this &#8220;shield&#8221; (<xref rid="B114" ref-type="bibr">Monreal et al., 2003</xref>; <xref rid="B160" ref-type="bibr">Soler-Llor&#233;ns et al., 2014</xref>). Additionally, while some rough vaccine candidates are not as protective against smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. as is S19 in mice, the difference may largely be accounted for by the activity of anti-O-PS antibodies, a component of the immune response that appears important in mice (depending on route of infection) but is of controversial significance in the natural hosts (<xref rid="B114" ref-type="bibr">Monreal et al., 2003</xref>). In <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> vaccine studies, the rough vaccine <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> 115 (<italic toggle="yes">wzm</italic> mutation) provides similar levels of protection against both <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> while <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis &#916;abcBA</italic> affords comparable protection against <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis, B. ovis</italic>, and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> (<xref rid="B1" ref-type="bibr">Adone et al., 2008</xref>; <xref rid="B41" ref-type="bibr">Costa et al., 2020</xref>; <xref rid="B50" ref-type="bibr">Eckstein et al., 2020</xref>). Nevertheless, some caveats must be pointed out. First, certain rough LAV candidates such as <italic toggle="yes">B. melitensis</italic> B115 still produce partial or complete O-PS that remains within the cytoplasm and the possibility that this internal O-PS assists in mediating protection cannot be ruled out. Second, some studies administered rough vaccine candidates at a higher dose than smooth candidates, as is the case with the recommended doses of RB51 vs. S19 in mice (10<sup>8</sup> CFU vs. 10<sup>5</sup> CFU) (<xref rid="B1" ref-type="bibr">Adone et al., 2008</xref>, <xref rid="B2" ref-type="bibr">2011</xref>; <xref rid="B5" ref-type="bibr">Arag&#243;n-Aranda et al., 2020</xref>). Despite this, these studies, although still few in number, indicate that a rough vaccine has the potential to protect against both smooth and rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp. and deserve further investigation. Advantages would include the oft-cited lack of interference with current serologic assays detecting anti-O-PS antibodies in addition to the possibility of producing an LAV on a background strain that is either non-zoonotic (<italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>) or exhibits reduced virulence for humans (<italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>).</p><p>As is known for development of vaccines against smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., there are limitations to the mouse model for development of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> vaccines and there is a significant need for more work in the natural hosts for these rough organisms, particularly <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>. As mentioned above, RB51 is moderately protective against <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> in mice but has no efficacy in sheep likely due to as yet incompletely understood differences in the immune response and/or differences in challenge route utilized between these two hosts (<xref rid="B87" ref-type="bibr">Jim&#233;nez de Bag&#252;&#233;s et al., 1994</xref>, <xref rid="B86" ref-type="bibr">1995</xref>). On the other hand, certain vaccines such as <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic>&#916;<italic toggle="yes">abcBA</italic> provide minimal protection in the mouse but are highly effective against experimental <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> infection in rams (<xref rid="B153" ref-type="bibr">Silva A. P. et al., 2015</xref>; <xref rid="B154" ref-type="bibr">Silva T. M. et al., 2015</xref>). Of additional concern, while Rev.1 can serve as a reference vaccine for comparison purposes in <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> vaccine studies, no such reference is available for <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>.</p></sec><sec id="S7"><title>Concluding Remarks</title><p>Conspicuously lacking in O-PS, <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> are virulent pathogens in their natural hosts that are able to recapitulate many of the properties and cellular interactions observed by their more famous smooth cousins. The lack of a vaccine for dogs and the recent rise in detection of <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> infection along with the negative economic impact of <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> on sheep production necessitates a more thorough understanding of these rough organisms and their interactions with the host. Oftentimes in complete contrast to what occurs with rough mutants of smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., these natural rough strains are able to evade and manipulate the host immune system by exhibiting low endotoxic activity, resisting destruction by complement and antimicrobial peptides, entering and trafficking within host cells along a similar pathway, and interfering with MHC-II antigen presentation. <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> appear to have compensated for the loss of O-PS by alterations to their outer membrane, particularly in regards to Omps. Whether this loss also serves an evolutionary advantage, as it does for other rough Gram-negative bacteria such as <italic toggle="yes">Y. pestis</italic>, is uncertain although the lower proinflammatory profile induced <italic toggle="yes">in vitro</italic> and in mice is suggestive of an enhanced level of stealth that could allow these pathogens to persist for long periods of time undetected. Given the commonalities in behavior between natural rough and smooth <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., it is not surprising that the immune response required to achieve protection against either phenotype appears similar. Nevertheless, much additional work is required to understand the correlates of immune protection against the natural rough <italic toggle="yes">Brucella</italic> spp., both in the natural host and involving mucosal routes of infection. Finally, the mouse model is useful in supporting the continued search for a vaccine against <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic> and evidence so far points to the superior protection of LAVs. Despite RB51&#8217;s reduced efficacy compared to S19 and Rev.1, there is evidence that a rough vaccine could serve to protect sheep and dogs against not only <italic toggle="yes">B. ovis</italic> and <italic toggle="yes">B. canis</italic>, but against smooth strains as well.</p></sec><sec id="S8"><title>Author Contributions</title><p>LS and AA-G contributed to the conception of the review. LS drafted the manuscript. Both authors reviewed and approved the manuscript.</p></sec><sec sec-type="COI-statement" id="conf1"><title>Conflict of Interest</title><p>The authors declare that the research was conducted in the absence of any commercial or financial relationships that could be construed as a potential conflict of interest.</p></sec></body><back><fn-group><fn
